### transformers — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — trust_remote_code=True Usage Without Sufficient Guardrails
  > The skill instructs users to load models with trust_remote_code=True for gated or custom architectures. This parameter allows arbitrary Python code bundled with a Hub model to execute on the user's machine. While the instructions note 'only when the model card requires custom code you have reviewed', this guidance is weak and may be ignored. Malicious or compromised Hub models could exploit this to execute arbitrary code.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Strengthen the warning around trust_remote_code=True. Add explicit guidance that this should be treated as executing untrusted code, recommend sandboxing or reviewing the model's custom code files before loading, and consider adding a note that this should never be used with models from unknown or unverified authors.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — HF_TOKEN Environment Variable Exposure Risk
  > The skill instructs users to set HF_TOKEN as an environment variable for authentication. While the instructions include reasonable security guidance (e.g., 'never commit tokens to git'), the skill's compatibility notes and instructions normalize the use of environment-based token storage. The static analyzer flagged environment variable access with network calls across multiple files, which warrants review. However, the instructions themselves are largely appropriate and do not appear to exfiltrate credentials.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** The guidance is generally sound. Ensure no referenced scripts (transformers.py, huggingface_hub.py) that were not found actually harvest HF_TOKEN or other environment variables and transmit them externally. Verify all missing referenced files before deployment.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Pinned Dependencies with Future Version Risk
  > The skill pins specific package versions (transformers 5.12.0, huggingface_hub 1.19.0, etc.) which is good practice. However, it references transformers 5.12.0 as a 'June 2026' release, suggesting this skill was written for a future version that may not yet exist or may have changed. Additionally, the skill encourages loosening pins 'for exploratory work', which could expose users to supply chain risks from unpinned dependencies.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Verify all pinned versions exist on PyPI before deployment. Maintain strict version pins and do not encourage loosening them without explicit security review. Consider adding hash verification for critical packages.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE` — Missing Referenced Script Files May Introduce Hidden Capabilities
  > The skill references several files that were not found during analysis: templates/generation.md, assets/models.md, assets/tokenizers.md, assets/training.md, assets/generation.md, templates/pipelines.md, templates/models.md, templates/training.md, transformers.py, huggingface_hub.py, assets/pipelines.md, templates/tokenizers.md. Notably, transformers.py and huggingface_hub.py are Python scripts that could contain executable code with behaviors not visible in this analysis. The static analyzer flagged a cross-file exfiltration chain across 3 files, suggesting these missing scripts may be relevant.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Audit all missing referenced files before deploying this skill. Specifically review transformers.py and huggingface_hub.py for any network calls, environment variable harvesting, or data exfiltration patterns. The static analyzer's cross-file exfiltration chain finding suggests these files may contain suspicious behavior.

### zarr-python — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Pre-Scan Flags Env Var Access with Network Calls Across Unreferenced Files
  > The static pre-scan analyzer detected BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION and BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_EXFILTRATION_CHAIN across 2 files in the skill package (33 total files: 16 markdown, 5 Python, 12 other). The SKILL.md and referenced markdown files provided for review contain no such code, but the package inventory includes 5 Python files and 12 other files that were not surfaced in the analysis input. These unreferenced scripts may contain environment variable harvesting combined with outbound network calls — a classic data exfiltration pattern. The cross-file chain finding suggests a multi-step read→send pattern split across files to evade single-file review.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Audit all 5 Python files and 12 other files in the package for environment variable access (os.environ, os.getenv, dotenv reads) combined with outbound HTTP/socket calls. Require full disclosure of all package files before deployment. Reject the skill if any file reads credentials and transmits them externally.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Community Skill Impersonates Official Zarr Package
  > The SKILL.md explicitly states 'This skill is a community guide maintained by K-Dense Inc., not an official zarr-developers package.' However, the skill name is 'zarr-python' — identical to the official upstream library name — and the description makes no qualification about its unofficial status. Users or agent discovery mechanisms searching for Zarr support may activate this community skill believing it to be the official or authoritative source, potentially receiving subtly incorrect guidance or being exposed to any malicious behavior hidden in the unreferenced files.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Rename the skill to something clearly non-official (e.g., 'kdense-zarr-guide' or 'zarr-python-community-guide'). Add the unofficial disclaimer prominently to the YAML description field so it is visible during skill discovery.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Multiple Referenced Files Not Found in Package
  > The SKILL.md references several files that do not exist in the skill package: h5py.py, zarr.py, xarray.py, dask.py, templates/api_reference.md, templates/v3_migration.md, assets/api_reference.md, assets/api_reference.md, assets/v3_migration.md. The presence of Python filenames matching popular third-party libraries (zarr.py, h5py.py, xarray.py, dask.py) is particularly concerning — if these files were present, they could shadow the legitimate installed packages via Python's module resolution order, causing the agent to import malicious local modules instead of the real libraries. While currently absent, their listing as referenced files suggests they may have been intended to be included or could be added later.
  > File: `references/api_reference.md`
  > **Remediation:** Remove all references to non-existent files. Never include local Python files named after standard library or popular third-party packages (zarr.py, h5py.py, etc.) as these shadow legitimate imports. Audit the package to ensure no such shadowing files are added in future versions.

  > The instructions reference a file `adaptyv.py` which was not found in the skill package. If this file is expected to contain credential-handling or API interaction code, its absence means the skill's behavior cannot be fully audited. If the file is later added, it could introduce data exfiltration or credential theft risks without review.

  > **Remediation:** Ensure all referenced files are included in the skill package and audited before deployment. Remove references to non-existent files or document their optional nature clearly.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Broad Skill Activation Triggers in Description
  > The skill description includes an extensive list of activation triggers covering multiple import patterns, domain names, and broad topic areas (protein binding assays, BLI/SPR assays, thermostability assays, etc.). While these appear legitimate for the skill's purpose, the breadth of triggers — including code import patterns like `adaptyv`, `adaptyv_sdk`, `FoundryClient` and URL references — could cause the skill to activate in contexts where it is not strictly needed, potentially exposing API key handling logic unnecessarily.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Narrow the activation triggers to the most specific and necessary keywords. Avoid triggering on generic scientific terms that may overlap with unrelated workflows.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned GitHub Dependency Installation
  > The skill instructs users to install the `adaptyv-sdk` package directly from a GitHub repository without any version pin, commit hash, or tag. This means any future commit to the repository could introduce malicious or breaking code that would be silently installed. There is no integrity verification (e.g., hash pinning) for the installed package.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin the installation to a specific commit hash or tag, e.g.: `uv pip install "git+https://github.com/adaptyvbio/adaptyv-sdk.git@v0.1.0"` or `git+https://github.com/adaptyvbio/adaptyv-sdk.git@<commit-sha>`. Once the package is published to PyPI, prefer the PyPI version with a pinned version specifier.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE` — Missing Referenced API Endpoint Files
  > The skill references `references/api-endpoints.md`, `templates/api-endpoints.md`, and `assets/api-endpoints.md` in its instructions, but only one of these (`references/api-endpoints.md`) was found. The missing files could indicate an incomplete package or could be placeholders for future content that has not been reviewed. If these files are later populated with malicious instructions, they could manipulate agent behavior.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Audit and include all referenced files in the skill package. Remove references to files that do not exist, or clearly document which files are optional. Ensure any future additions to these files are reviewed before deployment.

While internal skill files are expected to be safe, the presence of files like 'matplotlib.py' and 'sklearn.py' with names matching popular Python libraries is unusual. If these files were present, they could shadow legitimate imports or contain malicious code. Their absence means the risk is currently theoretical, but the naming pattern warrants attention.

While a version range is specified (>=1.4,<2), this is not a fully pinned version and could allow installation of a compromised minor/patch release. The 'all_extras' flag installs a broad set of additional packages (PyTorch stack, etc.) without explicit version pinning for transitive dependencies.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin to an exact version (e.g., aeon[all_extras]==1.4.0) for production use. Consider using a lock file (e.g., uv lock) to pin all transitive dependencies. Document which extras are actually needed rather than installing all_extras by default.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Unbounded Async Task Wait with 600-Second Default Timeout
  > The skill documents and encourages use of wait_for_task() with a default max_wait_seconds of 600 seconds (10 minutes). While a timeout is present, this pattern in automated workflows could lead to prolonged resource consumption, especially if multiple async operations are chained or if the task never completes due to a Benchling-side issue. The skill notes this is relevant for 'long-running serverless handlers' which could cause Lambda timeout misalignment.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Ensure callers always set an appropriate max_wait_seconds aligned with their execution environment. Document that the default 600s may exceed serverless function timeouts and always handle WaitForTaskExpiredError explicitly.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Static Analyzer Flagged Environment Variable Access with Network Calls Pattern
  > The static pre-scan flagged multiple instances of 'BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION' (environment variable access with network calls detected) across multiple files, and a cross-file env var exfiltration chain across 7 files. The skill's instructions and reference files do correctly document scoped environment variable reads (only named keys, never iterating os.environ), and all network calls target the user's own Benchling tenant URL. However, the pattern of reading BENCHLING_API_KEY, BENCHLING_CLIENT_SECRET, and other credentials and then making network calls is inherently sensitive. The skill does not contain any hardcoded secrets or calls to third-party exfiltration endpoints in the reviewed content.
  > File: `references/authentication.md`
  > **Remediation:** The pattern is legitimate for this use case. Ensure that all network calls in any associated scripts target only the user-configured tenant URL. Add explicit validation that the tenant URL matches expected Benchling domain patterns (e.g., *.benchling.com) before making authenticated calls.

### bioservices — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing allowed-tools Restriction for Python Network Access
  > The manifest declares allowed-tools: [Read, Write, Edit, Bash] but the skill's primary functionality is Python-based (all scripts are .py files using bioservices library). Python is not listed in allowed-tools, yet the skill instructs the agent to run Python scripts that make extensive network calls to 40+ external bioinformatics APIs. This is a minor inconsistency between the declared tool restrictions and actual usage pattern, though the network calls are all to legitimate bioinformatics services.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add Python to the allowed-tools list to accurately reflect the skill's actual tool usage. This improves transparency and allows the agent runtime to properly enforce tool restrictions.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned bioservices Package Installation
  > The skill installs bioservices with a pinned version (bioservices==1.16.0) which is good practice. However, the bioservices package itself has transitive dependencies (requests, suds-py3, beautifulsoup4, etc.) that are not pinned. A compromised transitive dependency could introduce malicious behavior. This is a standard supply chain risk for any Python package installation.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Consider using a lockfile (uv lock / requirements.txt with hashes) to pin all transitive dependencies. For high-security environments, audit the full dependency tree of bioservices==1.16.0 before deployment.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Environment Variable Access with Network Calls (Legitimate NCBI Email Pattern)
  > The skill accesses the NCBI_EMAIL environment variable and passes it to NCBIblast.run() as a required contact email for NCBI API calls. This is a well-documented, legitimate requirement by NCBI for BLAST service identification. The static analyzer flagged this as potential exfiltration, but the email is sent to NCBI's official API endpoint (ebi.ac.uk/Tools/services/rest/ncbiblast), not to any attacker-controlled server. The env var is declared in the skill manifest under openclaw.envVars with a clear description. No credential theft or unauthorized exfiltration pattern is present.
  > File: `scripts/protein_analysis_workflow.py`
  > **Remediation:** This is a legitimate pattern. No remediation needed. The NCBI_EMAIL env var is properly declared in the manifest and used only for NCBI API identification as required by NCBI policy.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Unbounded BLAST Polling Loop with Fixed Timeout
  > The protein_analysis_workflow.py script polls for BLAST job completion in a while loop with a 5-minute (300 second) maximum wait and 5-second sleep intervals. While a timeout is present, the loop could consume resources for the full 5 minutes if a job hangs in a non-terminal state other than FINISHED or ERROR. The status check relies on the external NCBI service returning expected status strings; unexpected status values would cause the loop to run until timeout.
  > File: `scripts/protein_analysis_workflow.py:130`
  > **Remediation:** Add handling for additional terminal states (e.g., FAILED, DELETED) to break the loop early. Consider exposing the timeout as a configurable parameter. This is a minor concern given the bounded timeout.

### datamol — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Cloud Credential Environment Variable Exposure Warning
  > The SKILL.md instructions explicitly mention that cloud I/O backends read credentials from environment variables (AWS_ACCESS_KEY_ID, AWS_SECRET_ACCESS_KEY, AWS_DEFAULT_REGION, GOOGLE_APPLICATION_CREDENTIALS). While the skill notes these are passed locally via fsspec and not transmitted to third-party endpoints, the explicit enumeration of credential variable names in instructions could guide misuse. The static analyzer flagged environment variable access with network calls, which aligns with the cloud I/O functionality described. The skill does include a note to 'Scope credential access to the named provider variables only' and to confirm remote write paths with users, which are positive mitigations.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** The existing mitigations are reasonable. Consider adding explicit guidance to never log or print credential variable values, and to validate that remote write destinations are user-controlled before writing. The skill should also warn users to audit fsspec backend versions for known vulnerabilities.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — External URL Data Loading Without Validation Warning
  > The skill instructs the agent to read data from user-provided URLs and cloud paths (e.g., 'https://example.com/data.csv', 's3://bucket/compounds.sdf') without input validation. While the skill notes to 'confirm the destination before writing' and 'use cloud paths only when the user explicitly requests them', there is no guidance on validating or sanitizing URL inputs before passing them to dm.read_csv() or dm.read_sdf(). A malicious URL could potentially serve crafted molecular data files designed to exploit parsing vulnerabilities in RDKit/datamol.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add guidance to validate URL schemes and domains before passing user-provided paths to I/O functions. Warn that external molecular data files should be treated as untrusted input. Consider recommending that the agent confirm external URLs with the user before fetching.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned External Dependencies
  > The skill instructs users to install datamol, s3fs, and gcsfs without version pinning (e.g., 'uv pip install datamol'). Unpinned dependencies are vulnerable to supply chain attacks where a malicious version could be published to PyPI. The skill also references scipy and scikit-learn as third-party dependencies without version constraints. While this is common practice for documentation, it represents a supply chain risk in an agentic context where the agent may execute these install commands automatically.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin dependency versions in installation instructions (e.g., 'uv pip install datamol==0.12.5'). Consider providing a requirements.txt or pyproject.toml with pinned versions for reproducible and secure installations. At minimum, document the tested/verified version range.

  > The skill references several files (templates/api_reference.md, deepchem.py, templates/workflows.md, assets/workflows.md, sklearn.py, assets/api_reference.md) that do not exist in the package. While this is not a direct security threat, missing referenced files could cause the agent to look for them in unexpected locations or fall back to external sources.

### deeptools — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned conda Dependency Installation Path
  > The SKILL.md instructions recommend installing deepTools via conda without a pinned version: 'conda install -c conda-forge -c bioconda deeptools'. While the PyPI path uses a pinned version (deepTools==3.5.6), the conda path is unpinned and could resolve to a different or compromised version. This is a supply chain risk, particularly on shared HPC systems where the conda environment may be shared or manipulated.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin the conda installation to a specific version: 'conda install -c conda-forge -c bioconda deeptools=3.5.6'. Additionally, consider specifying a conda lock file or environment.yml with pinned versions for reproducibility and supply chain integrity.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing allowed-tools Declaration for Network-Capable Operations
  > The skill's allowed-tools field lists Read, Write, Edit, Bash. The generated bash scripts invoke external tools (samtools, deepTools binaries) via Bash, which is declared. However, the static pre-scan flags potential environment variable access with network calls across files. While the provided script content does not show explicit credential harvesting or network exfiltration in the reviewed files, the pre-scan indicates cross-file exfiltration chain signals across 2 files not fully visible in the submission. This warrants a low-severity note pending full file review.
  > File: `scripts/workflow_generator.py`
  > **Remediation:** Audit all 32 files in the skill package (only a subset was provided for review). Specifically inspect any Python scripts not shown here for os.environ access combined with requests/urllib calls. Ensure no credentials or environment variables are transmitted to external endpoints.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Generated Bash Scripts Invoke samtools Without Version Pin
  > The ATAC-seq workflow generator script calls 'samtools index' directly without any version check or pinned dependency. samtools is assumed to be present in PATH and could be a compromised or unexpected version. This is a minor supply chain/dependency risk in generated scripts.
  > File: `scripts/workflow_generator.py:338`
  > **Remediation:** Document the required samtools version in the skill manifest or README. Consider adding a version check at the top of generated scripts (e.g., 'samtools --version | grep expected_version') to fail fast if an unexpected version is present.

### depmap — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — External Data Download Without Integrity Verification
  > The skill instructs downloading large data files from external URLs (figshare.com, depmap.org) using requests without any checksum verification, signature validation, or integrity checks. The download_depmap_data function streams content directly to disk without verifying the authenticity or integrity of the downloaded files. A compromised CDN or man-in-the-middle attack could substitute malicious data files.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add checksum verification (SHA256) for downloaded files. Compare against known-good hashes published by DepMap. Use HTTPS strictly and consider pinning certificates for the download endpoints.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Static Analysis Flags Potential Environment Variable Exfiltration Chain
  > The pre-scan static analysis detected signals for environment variable access combined with network calls (BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION, BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_EXFILTRATION_CHAIN, BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION) across 2 files. While the visible SKILL.md content does not explicitly show this pattern, the static analyzer found 32 files (22 markdown, 10 Python) in the package, none of which were provided for review. The uninspected Python scripts may contain credential harvesting or exfiltration logic that is not visible in the provided content.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Conduct a full audit of all 10 Python scripts in the package. Specifically inspect any scripts that access os.environ, os.getenv, or environment variables alongside requests or other network calls. Ensure no credentials or environment data are transmitted to external endpoints.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned External Dependencies and Missing Package Provenance
  > The skill references external Python packages (requests, pandas, scipy, numpy) without specifying version pins. The referenced file 'scipy.py' is not found in the package, suggesting a missing or misreferenced dependency. Unpinned dependencies are susceptible to supply chain attacks where a malicious version of a package could be installed.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Specify exact version requirements for all dependencies (e.g., requests==2.31.0, pandas==2.1.0). Include a requirements.txt with pinned versions and hashes. Clarify the role of the missing scipy.py reference.

### diffdock — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned External Dependencies and Docker Image
  > The skill instructs users to install from the upstream DiffDock repository via 'conda env create --file environment.yml' and pull a Docker image 'rbgcsail/diffdock' without version pinning. The environment.yml is from an external GitHub repository (github.com/gcorso/DiffDock) and the Docker image tag is unspecified. This creates supply chain risk if the upstream repository or Docker image is compromised or updated with malicious content.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin the Docker image to a specific digest (e.g., rbgcsail/diffdock@sha256:...). Reference a specific git commit or tag when cloning (e.g., git clone --branch v1.1.3). Verify the environment.yml contents before execution.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Static Analyzer Flags Environment Variable Access with Network Calls
  > The pre-scan static analyzer flagged potential environment variable exfiltration and cross-file exfiltration chains across 2 files. However, reviewing the provided script content (prepare_batch_csv.py, analyze_results.py, setup_check.py), none of these scripts contain explicit environment variable harvesting combined with network calls. The flagged behavior may exist in unreferenced or non-provided scripts within the 32-file package. The provided scripts use os.path, os.getcwd(), and os.walk equivalents only for local file validation. Without access to all 32 files, this cannot be fully cleared.
  > File: `scripts/prepare_batch_csv.py`
  > **Remediation:** Audit all 10 Python scripts in the package for environment variable access (os.environ, os.getenv) combined with network calls (requests, urllib, socket). Ensure no script reads credentials or env vars and transmits them externally.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — Skill Instructs Agent to Read External SDF Files from User-Provided Paths Without Validation
  > The batch processing workflow accepts user-provided CSV files containing arbitrary file paths for protein PDB files and ligand SDF/MOL2 files. The prepare_batch_csv.py validation script checks if files exist but does not sanitize or restrict paths. A malicious user could provide paths to sensitive files (e.g., ~/.ssh/config formatted as SDF) which the agent would then read and potentially expose in output. The ligand_description field also accepts arbitrary file paths.
  > File: `scripts/prepare_batch_csv.py`
  > **Remediation:** Restrict file path validation to expected directories (e.g., within the working directory). Add path traversal checks to prevent access to files outside the intended data directory. Validate file extensions (.pdb, .sdf, .mol2) before accepting paths.

### esm — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Environment Variable Access with Network Calls - Legitimate API Key Pattern
  > The static analyzer flagged environment variable access combined with network calls. The SKILL.md explicitly instructs never to hardcode tokens and to use environment variables. The network calls go to documented, trusted endpoints (forge.evolutionaryscale.ai, biohub.ai). This is a legitimate, security-conscious design. However, the skill does not validate that the API host is not overridden by user input, which is a minor concern worth noting.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing allowed-tools and Compatibility Metadata
  > The SKILL.md manifest does not specify `allowed-tools` or `compatibility` fields. While these are optional per the agent skills spec, their absence means there are no declared restrictions on which agent tools this skill may invoke. Given that the skill's reference files contain code that performs file writes (saving PDB files, FASTA files, cluster assignments, etc.) and network calls, declaring allowed tools would improve transparency and enable enforcement of least-privilege access.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add `allowed-tools: [Python, Bash]` and a `compatibility` field to the SKILL.md manifest to make the skill's intended tool usage explicit and auditable.

  > The biohub-platform.md reference documents an optional installation path from GitHub for ESMFold2 features: `uv pip install "esm@git+https://github.com/Biohub/esm.git@<full-40-character-commit-sha>"`. The skill appropriately warns against floating branch installs and requires a full 40-character commit SHA. The primary installation path uses a pinned PyPI version (`esm==3.2.3`). The GitHub path is presented as an advanced/optional path with appropriate caveats. The risk is low but present since the GitHub repository provenance depends on the Biohub organization's security posture.
  > File: `references/biohub-platform.md`
  > **Remediation:** The guidance is already sound. Consider adding a note to verify the commit SHA against a signed release tag or the official changelog before use. The primary PyPI path (`esm==3.2.3`) should be preferred in automated contexts.

The comment in the code acknowledges this limitation.
  > File: `scripts/doe_designs.py:97`
  > **Remediation:** Use numpy.random.default_rng(seed) and pass the rng object if pyDOE3 supports it, or document the limitation clearly. This is a low-priority code quality issue.

### generate-image — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — API Key Read from Environment and .env File Sent to External API
  > The script reads the OPENROUTER_API_KEY from .env files traversed up the directory tree and uses it to authenticate requests to https://openrouter.ai. While this is the intended and documented behavior for this skill, the static analyzer flagged a cross-file env var exfiltration chain. In this context, the API key is legitimately used to call the OpenRouter API for image generation, and the destination (openrouter.ai) is a known commercial AI API provider. The behavior is transparent and documented. However, the .env traversal walks all parent directories up to the filesystem root, which could inadvertently pick up API keys from unrelated projects or sensitive directories.
  > File: `scripts/generate_image.py`
  > **Remediation:** Limit .env file search to the current directory and at most one or two parent directories rather than traversing all the way to the filesystem root. This prevents accidentally picking up API keys from unrelated projects. Consider also validating the API key format before use.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — User-Supplied Input Image Sent to External API Without Validation
  > When an input image is provided via --input, the script reads the file, base64-encodes it, and sends it to the OpenRouter API. There is no validation of the image content beyond checking the file extension for MIME type. A malicious or unexpected file could be read and transmitted. Additionally, the output path is user-controlled and not sanitized, which could allow path traversal (e.g., --output ../../sensitive_location.png).
  > File: `scripts/generate_image.py`
  > **Remediation:** Validate that the input image path is within an expected directory. Sanitize the output path to prevent path traversal. Consider adding file size limits to prevent reading extremely large files. Validate that the file is actually an image before encoding and transmitting.

  > The skill description is extremely broad, listing a large number of geospatial use cases (buffer analysis, spatial joins, dissolving boundaries, clipping, area/distance calculations, reprojecting, choropleth mapping, format conversion, PostGIS, folium, cartopy, matplotlib). While this reflects the genuine breadth of the geopandas library, the description could cause the agent to activate this skill for a very wide range of geographic data tasks, potentially beyond the user's intent. This is a minor concern given the skill is a legitimate library reference.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Consider scoping the description more narrowly to the primary use cases to reduce unintended activation. This is a low-priority concern for a legitimate library reference skill.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Dependencies in Installation Instructions
  > The skill instructs users to install multiple packages (geopandas, folium, mapclassify, pyarrow, psycopg2, geoalchemy2, contextily, cartopy) using 'uv pip install' without specifying version pins. Unpinned dependencies are vulnerable to supply chain attacks where a malicious package version could be published and automatically installed. This is a low severity concern as these are well-known packages, but version pinning is a security best practice.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin package versions in installation instructions (e.g., 'uv pip install geopandas==1.0.1') or provide a requirements.txt with pinned versions to ensure reproducible and secure installations.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — PostGIS Connection String Includes Credentials in Example Code
  > The data-io.md reference file contains an example PostGIS connection string that includes a username and password placeholder in the URL format: 'postgresql://user:password@host:port/database'. While these are placeholder values and not hardcoded real credentials, users following this pattern may inadvertently hardcode real credentials in their scripts. The skill does not warn users about credential management best practices.
  > File: `references/data-io.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add a note in the documentation advising users to use environment variables or a secrets manager for database credentials rather than hardcoding them in connection strings. Example: use os.environ.get('DB_PASSWORD') or a .env file with python-dotenv.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Remote URL Data Loading Without Security Warning
  > The data-io.md reference file demonstrates loading spatial data directly from remote URLs (HTTP/HTTPS and cloud storage like S3, Azure Blob) without any security warnings about validating the source or content of remote files. Loading data from untrusted URLs could expose the agent to malicious geospatial files or indirect prompt injection via crafted file content.
  > File: `references/data-io.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add a security note advising users to only load data from trusted sources and to validate file origins before loading. Consider noting that user-provided URLs should be treated as untrusted input.

### get-available-resources — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Referenced Files (dask.py, joblib.py, torch.py) Not Found in Package
  > The SKILL.md instructions reference files `dask.py`, `joblib.py`, and `torch.py` which are not present in the skill package. If these files were present and named identically to popular packages, they could constitute module shadowing attacks.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Overly Broad Activation Trigger in Skill Description
  > The skill description instructs the agent to use it 'at the start of ANY computationally intensive scientific task' and lists broad trigger scenarios. While not malicious, this broad activation language could cause the skill to be invoked more frequently than necessary, collecting and persisting system resource data on every scientific task regardless of actual need.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Narrow the activation criteria to specific user-initiated requests rather than broad automatic invocation. Consider making the trigger opt-in rather than proactive.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Subprocess Calls to External GPU Detection Tools Without Resource Limits
  > The script invokes `nvidia-smi`, `rocm-smi`, `sysctl`, and `system_profiler` via subprocess with a 5-10 second timeout. While timeouts are present, `system_profiler SPDisplaysDataType` on macOS can be slow and resource-intensive. Additionally, if these tools behave unexpectedly (e.g., hang before the timeout triggers), they could delay agent execution. The timeout handling is adequate but the 10-second timeout for `system_profiler` is relatively generous.
  > File: `scripts/detect_resources.py:130`
  > **Remediation:** Consider reducing the `system_profiler` timeout to 5 seconds consistent with other subprocess calls. Document that GPU detection may add latency on macOS systems.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — System Resource Information Written to Predictable File Path
  > The script writes detailed system resource information (CPU cores, memory, disk space, GPU details) to a predictable file path `.claude_resources.json` in the current working directory. While this is the stated purpose of the skill, the file contains potentially sensitive system fingerprinting data that could be read by other processes or skills. The file is not protected and persists on disk.
  > File: `scripts/detect_resources.py:175`
  > **Remediation:** Consider documenting that the output file contains system fingerprinting data. Optionally restrict file permissions after writing (e.g., chmod 600) or allow users to opt out of persisting the file.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — User-Controlled Output Path Passed to File Write Without Sanitization
  > The `-o/--output` argument from the command line is passed directly to `open()` without path sanitization or validation. A malicious caller could supply a path like `/etc/cron.d/malicious` or use path traversal sequences to write the JSON output to unintended locations. While the content written is structured JSON (not arbitrary code), writing to arbitrary paths is a path traversal risk.
  > File: `scripts/detect_resources.py:232`
  > **Remediation:** Validate and sanitize the output path. Restrict writes to the current working directory or a user-specified safe directory. Use `pathlib.Path(args.output).resolve()` and verify it stays within an expected base directory.

However, the skill references a very large number of internal files across `references/`, `assets/`, and `templates/` subdirectories. Many of these files were not found during analysis (marked as 'not found'). While reading internal files is expected behavior, the large number of missing referenced files could indicate incomplete packaging or potential for unexpected behavior if the agent attempts to read non-existent files.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Ensure all referenced files are included in the skill package. Remove references to files that do not exist, or document that they are optional. This prevents the agent from attempting to read missing files and producing errors or unexpected behavior.

  > The skill references 19 files across assets/, templates/, and references/ directories, but many are not found (assets/coverage.md, templates/overlap.md, gtars.py, templates/coverage.md, templates/refget.md, assets/tokenizers.md, assets/cli.md, templates/python-api.md, templates/cli.md, assets/overlap.md, templates/tokenizers.md, assets/refget.md, assets/python-api.md). - **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Installation Without Version Constraints
  > The skill instructs installation of 'gtars' via 'uv pip install gtars' and 'cargo install gtars-cli' without specifying version pins. This exposes users to supply chain risks where a compromised or malicious package version could be installed automatically. The Rust/Cargo installation also uses feature flags without version pinning.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin exact versions for all package installations: 'uv pip install gtars==<exact_version>' and 'cargo install gtars-cli --version <exact_version>'. Use lock files (Cargo.lock, uv.lock) to ensure reproducible builds.

  > File: `SKILL.md`

  > The skill instructs users to install the 'hypogenic' package without pinning a specific version (e.g., 'uv pip install hypogenic'). This means any future malicious or compromised version published to PyPI could be installed automatically, creating a supply chain risk. Additionally, the skill clones external GitHub repositories (ChicagoHAI/HypoGeniC-datasets, ChicagoHAI/Hypothesis-agent-datasets) without specifying commit hashes or tags, which could allow supply chain compromise if those repositories are taken over.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin the package to a specific version (e.g., 'uv pip install hypogenic==1.0.0') and pin git clones to specific commit hashes or tags to ensure reproducibility and prevent supply chain attacks.

Combined with the instruction to generate 50+ page reports with no token constraints, this creates a pattern of potentially unbounded compute and time consumption. The SKILL.md explicitly states 'no token constraints' and encourages generating all visuals before writing.
  > File: `scripts/generate_market_visuals.py:155`
  > **Remediation:** Add a maximum visual count limit without explicit user confirmation. Implement a total timeout budget rather than per-image timeout only. Warn users about resource implications before batch generation.

  > The static analyzer flagged a Python code block using eval/exec. Upon review, the references/language.md file contains a Nextflow DSL2 code example that uses `eval()` as a Nextflow process output qualifier (e.g., `eval('samtools --version | sed ...')`) to capture tool version strings. This is a Nextflow-specific language construct for capturing command output, not a Python eval/exec call for arbitrary code execution. The context is clearly educational/documentation, not executable Python. The risk is minimal but noted for completeness.
  > File: `references/developing.md`
  > **Remediation:** No action required. The eval() usage is a Nextflow DSL2 output qualifier for capturing command stdout, not Python code execution. Documentation is accurate and appropriate for the skill's purpose.

### parallel-web — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — Indirect Prompt Injection via Untrusted Web Content
  > The skill fetches and processes content from external web sources (search results, extracted pages, research reports, monitor events, enrichment values). The instructions do include warnings to treat returned content as untrusted and to ignore embedded instructions, but the agent is still exposed to indirect prompt injection through web-sourced data that may contain adversarial instructions designed to manipulate agent behavior.
  > **Remediation:** The skill already includes appropriate warnings. Consider adding explicit instructions to sandbox or quote web-sourced content before presenting it to the user, and to never act on instructions found in web content regardless of how they are framed. The mitigations present are good practice but cannot fully prevent indirect injection.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Potential Command Injection via User-Supplied Text in Shell Arguments
  > The skill instructs the agent to pass user text as quoted shell arguments to parallel-cli commands. While the instructions warn against concatenating raw user text into JSON or shell commands and recommend using stdin for shell-sensitive input, the agent must correctly implement this guidance. If the agent interpolates user-supplied text directly into shell command strings rather than using proper argument passing, command injection is possible.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** The instructions are appropriately cautious. Ensure the agent execution environment enforces argument passing via arrays rather than shell string interpolation. Consider adding explicit examples showing safe stdin usage patterns for all capabilities, not just search and research.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — API Key Exposure Risk via Environment Variable Handling
  > The skill references PARALLEL_API_KEY and instructs the agent to check for its presence in .env files. While the instructions say 'Do not inspect an entire .env file; if credential presence must be checked, look only for the PARALLEL_API_KEY key name and never display its value,' this guidance relies on the agent following instructions correctly. The API key is also passed implicitly through the CLI environment, and any logging or verbose output from the CLI could inadvertently expose it.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** The instructions are reasonably cautious. Consider adding explicit guidance to suppress verbose CLI output that might echo environment variables, and to verify that parallel-cli does not log the API key in debug mode.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Upgrade Path
  > The skill installs parallel-web-tools at a pinned version (0.7.1) for initial installation, which is good practice. However, the upgrade command 'uv tool upgrade parallel-web-tools' has no version pin, meaning an upgrade could install an untrusted or compromised version of the package without verification.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Consider pinning the upgrade target to a specific verified version, or at minimum documenting the expected version after upgrade. Add a post-upgrade verification step (e.g., checking the version hash or changelog) before use.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Webhook URL May Expose Sensitive Endpoint Information
  > The monitor capability supports webhook delivery to user-supplied HTTPS endpoints. The instructions note 'Do not place credentials in the webhook URL,' but the webhook URL itself (which may contain internal infrastructure details) is logged and reported back to the user. Additionally, monitor events delivered to webhooks contain web-sourced data that should be treated as untrusted.
  > File: `references/monitor.md`
  > **Remediation:** Ensure webhook URLs are validated as user-authorized HTTPS endpoints before use. Consider masking or truncating webhook URLs in reports to avoid exposing internal infrastructure details in logs or agent output.

### pptx — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Over-Broad Skill Activation Description
  > The skill description is extremely broad, instructing the agent to activate 'any time a .pptx file is involved in any way' and to trigger on generic words like 'deck,' 'slides,' or 'presentation.' This over-broad activation scope could cause the skill to be invoked in contexts where it is not needed, potentially consuming resources or interfering with other skills. While not malicious, this represents capability inflation through keyword baiting.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Narrow the activation criteria to specific, well-defined use cases rather than triggering on any mention of presentation-related terms.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Dependency Versions
  > The SKILL.md dependencies section specifies packages without version pins (e.g., 'pip install markitdown[pptx]', 'pip install Pillow', 'npm install -g pptxgenjs'). Unpinned dependencies are vulnerable to supply chain attacks where a malicious version of a package could be published and automatically installed. This is a low-severity concern for a local skill package but represents a supply chain risk.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin all dependencies to specific versions (e.g., 'pip install markitdown[pptx]==0.x.x', 'pip install Pillow==10.x.x') and consider using a lockfile (requirements.txt with hashes, package-lock.json) to ensure reproducible installations.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Dynamic C Code Compilation and LD_PRELOAD Injection
  > The soffice.py script contains embedded C source code (_SHIM_SOURCE) that is compiled at runtime using gcc and loaded via LD_PRELOAD. While this appears to be a legitimate workaround for sandboxed environments where AF_UNIX sockets are blocked, the pattern of compiling and injecting native code at runtime is inherently risky. If the temp directory is writable by an attacker, or if the skill is used in an unexpected context, this mechanism could be abused. The _exit(0) call in the shim's close() function is also notable as it terminates the process unconditionally.
  > File: `scripts/office/soffice.py`
  > **Remediation:** Document clearly why this shim is needed and under what conditions it activates. Consider adding integrity checks or restricting the shim to only activate in explicitly sandboxed environments. Ensure the temp directory used for compilation is not world-writable.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Environment Variable Access in soffice.py
  > The soffice.py script calls os.environ.copy() to copy the entire process environment and passes it to subprocess calls. While this is a common pattern for subprocess invocation, it means all environment variables (which may include secrets, API keys, tokens, or other sensitive data) are passed to the soffice subprocess. The static analyzer flagged this as environment variable access combined with subprocess execution. In this context it appears to be legitimate LibreOffice configuration rather than exfiltration, but it does expose the full environment to an external process.
  > File: `scripts/office/soffice.py`
  > **Remediation:** Consider constructing a minimal environment dictionary with only the variables needed by LibreOffice rather than copying the entire process environment. This reduces the attack surface if LibreOffice itself is compromised.

### pydicom — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Dependencies in Installation Instructions
  > The SKILL.md installation instructions use unpinned package versions (e.g., 'uv pip install pydicom', 'uv pip install pillow', 'uv pip install numpy', 'uv pip install pylibjpeg'). Without version pinning, the skill is vulnerable to supply chain attacks where a compromised or malicious version of a dependency could be installed. This is particularly concerning in a medical imaging context where data integrity is critical.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin all dependencies to specific versions (e.g., 'uv pip install pydicom==2.4.4 pillow==10.2.0 numpy==1.26.4'). Consider using a requirements.txt or pyproject.toml with locked versions and hash verification.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Incomplete DICOM Anonymization - Missing PHI Tags
  > The anonymize_dicom.py script and the SKILL.md anonymization example do not cover all DICOM tags that may contain PHI. Notably absent from the PHI_TAGS list are tags such as AccessionNumber, StudyID, RequestedProcedureID, ScheduledProcedureStepID, and private tags that vendors may use to store patient information. Additionally, UIDs (StudyInstanceUID, SeriesInstanceUID, SOPInstanceUID) are explicitly commented out, which can allow re-identification of patients through UID correlation across datasets.
  > File: `scripts/anonymize_dicom.py`
  > **Remediation:** Document clearly that UID anonymization is required for full de-identification. Add AccessionNumber, StudyID, and other potentially identifying tags to PHI_TAGS. Consider implementing DICOM PS 3.15 Annex E (De-identification profiles) for compliance. Warn users that the current implementation may not be sufficient for HIPAA-compliant de-identification.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — PHI Exposure Risk in Metadata Extraction Script
  > The extract_metadata.py script extracts and displays all DICOM metadata including Protected Health Information (PHI) such as PatientName, PatientID, PatientBirthDate, PatientSex, PatientAge, PatientWeight, and other sensitive fields. When output is written to a file (--output flag), this PHI could be written to disk in plaintext without any access controls or warnings about the sensitivity of the data. The script does not warn users about PHI exposure risks.
  > File: `scripts/extract_metadata.py`
  > **Remediation:** Add a warning to the script about PHI sensitivity when outputting to files. Consider adding a --redact-phi flag that masks sensitive fields by default. Document HIPAA/GDPR compliance considerations in the script's help text.

### pyhealth — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing License and Compatibility Metadata
  > The skill manifest does not specify a license or compatibility field. While this is LOW severity per the analysis framework (these fields are optional), the absence of provenance information (no license) combined with an unknown author ('K-Dense Inc.') and no compatibility declaration reduces auditability and trust. Users cannot easily determine the terms under which the skill operates or which environments it has been validated for.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add a license field (e.g., MIT, Apache-2.0) and a compatibility field specifying supported environments. This improves transparency and auditability.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Over-Broad Activation Triggers in Skill Description
  > The skill description and SKILL.md 'When to use this skill' section contain an extensive list of activation keywords and explicitly instructs the agent to activate 'even if PyHealth isn't named explicitly.' This broad trigger language could cause the skill to activate in contexts where it is not appropriate, inflating its perceived scope and increasing unwanted activation frequency. While the skill appears legitimate, the explicit instruction to activate without the primary keyword being mentioned is a mild capability inflation pattern.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Narrow the activation criteria to require more specific signals. Avoid instructing the agent to activate without the primary skill keyword being present. Limit trigger keywords to those directly relevant to the skill's core functionality.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned External Package Dependency
  > The skill instructs users to install PyHealth via 'uv add pyhealth' without pinning to a specific version (except for the legacy 1.x case). While uv generates a lockfile (uv.lock) that pins transitive dependencies for a given project, the skill's instructions do not emphasize verifying the lockfile or pinning the top-level pyhealth version. This means a compromised or malicious future release of pyhealth could be pulled in by users following the instructions without a pinned version specifier.
  > File: `references/installation.md`
  > **Remediation:** Recommend pinning the pyhealth version explicitly (e.g., 'uv add pyhealth==2.x.y') and committing the uv.lock file. Document how to verify the package integrity.

### pylabrobot — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Multiple Referenced Files Not Found in Package
  > The skill references numerous files in its instructions that are not present in the package: assets/visualization.md, pylabrobot.py, assets/resources.md, templates/material-handling.md, templates/analytical-equipment.md, assets/liquid-handling.md, templates/hardware-backends.md, templates/liquid-handling.md, assets/material-handling.md, assets/analytical-equipment.md, assets/hardware-backends.md, templates/visualization.md, templates/resources.md. The absence of these files means the agent may attempt to locate or load them from unexpected locations, or the skill is incomplete. The static analyzer also flagged potential cross-file exfiltration chains involving 2 files, which warrants attention given the missing files.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Ensure all referenced files are included in the skill package. Remove references to files that do not exist, or document why they are absent. Audit the pylabrobot.py file (referenced but not found) especially carefully if it is later added, as it could contain executable code.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing allowed-tools Metadata
  > The skill does not specify the 'allowed-tools' field in its YAML manifest. While this is optional per the agent skills spec, it means there are no declared restrictions on which agent tools (Read, Write, Bash, Python, etc.) can be used. For a skill that controls physical laboratory hardware, documenting intended tool usage would improve transparency and security posture.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add an explicit 'allowed-tools' field to the YAML manifest listing only the tools required for the skill's operation (e.g., [Python, Read, Write]).

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing Compatibility Metadata
  > The skill does not specify the 'compatibility' field in its YAML manifest. Given that this skill controls physical laboratory hardware (Hamilton STAR, Opentrons OT-2, Tecan EVO, etc.), documenting platform compatibility and hardware requirements would improve transparency.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add a 'compatibility' field to the YAML manifest specifying supported platforms and hardware requirements.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Installation Recommendation
  > The SKILL.md Quick Start section recommends installing PyLabRobot using 'uv pip install pylabrobot' without specifying a version pin. This means the agent or user could install any version of the package, including potentially compromised future versions. For a skill controlling physical laboratory hardware, supply chain integrity is important.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin the package to a specific known-good version (e.g., 'uv pip install pylabrobot==0.x.y') and document the expected version in the manifest. Consider adding a hash verification step.

### pymc — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Missing Version Pins for Optional Dependencies
  > The SKILL.md compatibility note recommends pinning PyMC to 6.0.1 via `uv pip install "pymc[nutpie]==6.0.1"`, which is good practice. However, optional samplers (NumPyro, BlackJAX) and their JAX-compatible dependencies are described as 'separately pinned' without providing explicit pinned versions in the skill package itself. This leaves a supply chain gap where unpinned transitive dependencies could be substituted with compromised versions.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Provide a complete lockfile (e.g., uv.lock or requirements.txt with exact hashes) for all optional dependency chains, including JAX, NumPyro, and BlackJAX, within the skill package.

### pytdc — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing allowed-tools Declaration
  > The skill does not declare an allowed-tools field in its YAML manifest. While this is optional per the spec, the skill executes Python scripts that make network calls (downloading datasets from TDC servers) and writes data to local directories (e.g., 'data/' path in benchmark_evaluation.py). Declaring allowed-tools would improve transparency about the skill's capabilities.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add 'allowed-tools: [Python, Bash]' to the YAML frontmatter to explicitly declare the tools this skill uses.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing Compatibility Field
  > The skill does not specify a compatibility field in its YAML manifest. This makes it unclear in which agent environments the skill is intended to operate, potentially leading to unexpected behavior or activation in incompatible contexts.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add a compatibility field specifying the intended agent environments, e.g., 'compatibility: Claude.ai, Claude Code, API'.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned PyTDC Dependency Installation
  > The skill instructs installation of PyTDC without a pinned version number. The install command 'uv pip install PyTDC' and 'uv pip install PyTDC --upgrade' do not specify a version, meaning a compromised or malicious future version of the package could be installed. PyTDC downloads datasets from external servers at runtime, which further extends the supply chain trust boundary.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin the PyTDC version to a known-good release, e.g., 'uv pip install PyTDC==0.3.6'. Avoid using --upgrade in automated contexts without version validation.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Runtime Network Data Downloads Without User Confirmation
  > The scripts automatically download large datasets from TDC's external servers (e.g., BindingDB_Kd with 991,486 pairs, ChEMBL_V29, USPTO with 1.9M reactions) when dataset objects are instantiated. This occurs silently without explicit user confirmation of the data transfer. The pre-scan flagged cross-file network behavior. While this is expected behavior for a data science library, the skill does not inform users that significant data will be downloaded from external servers.
  > File: `scripts/load_and_split_data.py:30`
  > **Remediation:** Add explicit documentation in SKILL.md warning users that dataset instantiation triggers downloads from external TDC servers. Consider adding user confirmation prompts before initiating large downloads.

### pyzotero — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Environment Variable Access for API Credentials
  > The skill accesses ZOTERO_API_KEY, ZOTERO_LIBRARY_ID, and ZOTERO_LIBRARY_TYPE environment variables to authenticate with the Zotero Web API. While this is the documented and expected behavior for this skill, the static analyzer flagged cross-file environment variable access patterns combined with network calls. The credentials are passed to the pyzotero library which makes outbound HTTPS calls to api.zotero.org. This is legitimate and expected behavior for a Zotero API client skill, but users should be aware that their API key is transmitted over the network to Zotero's servers.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** This is expected behavior for the skill's stated purpose. Ensure the ZOTERO_API_KEY is scoped with minimum required permissions (read-only if write access is not needed). The authentication reference file already advises against hardcoding credentials, which is good practice.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — File Download to Arbitrary Paths
  > The files-attachments reference documents zot.dump() which can write downloaded attachment files to arbitrary filesystem paths specified by the user or agent. While this is legitimate functionality for a Zotero client, it could be used to write files to sensitive locations if the path parameter is not validated.
  > File: `references/files-attachments.md`
  > **Remediation:** When using zot.dump() with user-provided paths, validate that the target path is within an expected directory. Avoid writing to system directories or paths outside the user's project workspace.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Unbounded Collection Traversal Pattern
  > The skill documents patterns using zot.everything() which makes unlimited sequential API calls to retrieve all items from a library. For large Zotero libraries with thousands of items, this could result in excessive API calls, rate limiting, and prolonged execution. The pagination reference explicitly notes 'large libraries may take time'.
  > File: `references/pagination.md`
  > **Remediation:** When using everything(), consider adding progress feedback to the user and implementing reasonable limits. Use since=version parameter for incremental sync workflows rather than fetching all items repeatedly. The error-handling reference already shows rate-limit retry logic which should be used.

  > The skill description claims Qiskit has '13M+ downloads', '83x faster transpilation than competitors', and '29% fewer two-qubit gates' — marketing claims that inflate perceived capability. The description also references IonQ and Amazon Braket support beyond the stated IBM Quantum focus, which could cause unintended activation for non-IBM quantum computing queries.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Dependencies

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — API Token Handling in Reference Documentation
  > The setup reference file (references/setup.md) includes instructions for saving IBM Quantum API tokens using QiskitRuntimeService.save_account() and also suggests setting tokens via environment variables (QISKIT_IBM_TOKEN). While this is standard Qiskit usage, the documentation uses placeholder strings like 'YOUR_IBM_QUANTUM_TOKEN' which could lead users to inadvertently expose credentials if they follow examples carelessly. The backends.md also shows token usage patterns.
  > File: `references/setup.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add explicit warnings in documentation that API tokens should never be hardcoded in scripts. Recommend using environment variables or credential managers rather than inline token strings.

### rdkit — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Pickle Deserialization Warning Present but No Enforcement
  > The SKILL.md instructions include a warning about not loading Python pickle files from untrusted sources, noting that pickle deserialization can execute arbitrary code. While the skill correctly warns against this and recommends safer alternatives (SMILES/SDF/RDKit binary), there is no enforcement mechanism. The skill's scripts accept arbitrary file paths from user input (e.g., database files in similarity_search.py and molecular_properties.py), and if a user were to pass a pickle file, the warning alone would not prevent execution. This is a documentation-level concern rather than a code-level vulnerability, but worth noting.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** The warning is appropriate and well-placed. Consider adding explicit file extension validation in the scripts to reject .pkl/.pickle files, reinforcing the documented guidance programmatically.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Referenced File 'rdkit.py' Not Found in Package
  > The SKILL.md instructions reference a file 'rdkit.py' in the referenced files section, but this file was not found in the skill package. This creates a discrepancy between the manifest's claimed contents and the actual package contents. While this appears to be a documentation/packaging error rather than a deliberate deception, it could cause confusion or unexpected behavior when the agent attempts to load this file.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Either include the rdkit.py file in the skill package or remove the reference to it from the SKILL.md instructions. Ensure the package contents match what is documented.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Unvalidated File Path Arguments Accepted from User Input
  > The three scripts (similarity_search.py, molecular_properties.py, substructure_filter.py) accept file paths directly from command-line arguments without path traversal validation. While this is standard CLI tool behavior and the scripts do check for file existence, there is no restriction preventing paths like ../../sensitive_file or absolute paths outside the working directory. In an agentic context where the agent constructs these arguments based on user input, this could allow access to files outside the intended working scope.
  > File: `scripts/similarity_search.py:97`
  > **Remediation:** Add path validation to ensure input files are within expected directories. Consider using Path.resolve() and checking that the resolved path is within an allowed base directory before opening files.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — SMARTS Pattern Input Passed Directly to RDKit Without Sanitization
  > In substructure_filter.py, user-supplied SMARTS/SMILES patterns from command-line arguments are passed directly to Chem.MolFromSmarts() and Chem.MolFromSmiles() without any pre-validation. While RDKit itself handles malformed patterns gracefully (returning None), extremely complex or pathological SMARTS patterns could potentially cause excessive computation. This is a low-severity concern as RDKit's C++ layer provides inherent protection, but the pattern is worth noting in an agentic context where patterns may be constructed from user input.
  > File: `scripts/substructure_filter.py:88`
  > **Remediation:** Consider adding a length limit on pattern strings and logging when patterns fail to parse. For agentic use, validate that pattern strings come from trusted sources before passing to RDKit.

Upon review, the flagged content appears within the references/nstc_guidelines.md file in a LaTeX/bash installation example block, not in executable Python scripts. The code block shows 'latex nstc-proposal.ins' which is a LaTeX command, not Python eval/exec. No actual Python scripts are present in this skill package. The finding is a false positive from the static analyzer pattern matching on documentation content, but is noted for completeness.
  > File: `references/nstc_guidelines.md`
  > **Remediation:** No action required. This is documentation content showing LaTeX installation commands, not executable Python code with eval/exec. The static analyzer produced a false positive.

This is a minor supply chain concern in documentation context only, as no automated installation occurs.
  > File: `references/nstc_guidelines.md`
  > **Remediation:** Consider adding version pins or commit hashes to the GitHub clone instructions (e.g., 'git clone --branch v1.0 ...' or specifying a commit hash). Add a note that users should verify the integrity of community-contributed templates before use.

### scientific-critical-thinking — 🔵 LOW

  > The SKILL.md instructions document that the optional scientific-schematics skill sends user-provided prompt text to OpenRouter, a third-party API. While this is disclosed to the user in the instructions, the skill's own manifest does not fully reflect this network dependency (it is noted only in the compatibility field). The disclosure is present and appropriate, but users should be aware that prompt content is transmitted externally when this optional feature is used.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** The disclosure is already present and appropriate. No remediation required beyond ensuring users read the disclosure before using the optional schematic generation feature. The compatibility field already notes the OPENROUTER_API_KEY requirement.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Pre-Scan Flags for Environment Variable Exfiltration Not Confirmed in Reviewed Files
  > The static pre-scan context reports findings of BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION and BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_EXFILTRATION_CHAIN across Python files. However, no Python script files were provided for review in this skill package (the skill reports 'No script files found'). The referenced scripts (e.g., scripts/generate_schematic.py) belong to the companion scientific-schematics skill, not this skill. The OPENROUTER_API_KEY environment variable access is expected behavior for the optional schematic generation feature and is disclosed. Without access to the actual Python scripts, the pre-scan flags cannot be fully validated or dismissed.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Provide the Python script files (scripts/generate_schematic.py and any related scripts) for full review. Verify that environment variable access is limited to OPENROUTER_API_KEY for the disclosed API call and that no other sensitive environment variables (e.g., AWS credentials, SSH keys) are accessed or transmitted.

### statistical-power — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing Referenced Files May Lead to Untrusted Substitution
  > Several files referenced in the SKILL.md instructions are not found in the skill package: assets/effect_sizes.md, assets/closed_form_recipes.md, templates/effect_sizes.md, templates/simulation_based_power.md, templates/closed_form_recipes.md, assets/simulation_based_power.md, simulate_power.py (root), and power.py (root). While the canonical versions exist under references/ and scripts/, the missing duplicates could be substituted by a user or attacker placing malicious files at those paths. The risk is low since the instructions primarily reference the correct paths, but the ambiguity in path resolution is worth noting.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Remove ambiguous or duplicate path references from SKILL.md. Ensure all referenced files use consistent, canonical paths (e.g., references/ and scripts/) and that no alternative path locations are implied.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Versions in Installation Instructions
  > The installation instructions in SKILL.md use minimum-version specifiers (>=) rather than exact pins for all packages. While the compatibility note acknowledges this and says 'unpinned is fine for exploration,' the Bash tool is allowed and could execute these install commands. Unpinned dependencies introduce supply chain risk: a compromised or malicious future version of statsmodels, scipy, pingouin, numpy, matplotlib, pandas, or lifelines could be installed without the user's awareness.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Unbounded Loop in find_sample_size May Cause Compute Exhaustion
  > The find_sample_size() function in scripts/simulate_power.py contains an unbounded expansion loop that doubles 'hi' until power reaches the target or hi >= 1,000,000. Combined with the inner bisection loop and the simulate_power() call (which itself runs n_sims iterations), this can result in very large numbers of model fits (e.g., mixed models, logistic regression) being executed. For slow models or adversarially chosen parameters, this could exhaust compute resources or run for an extremely long time.
  > File: `scripts/simulate_power.py:52`
  > **Remediation:** Add an explicit iteration limit to the outer expansion loop and the bisection loop. Log a warning if the limit is reached. Consider adding a timeout mechanism or requiring the caller to specify a reasonable upper bound.

  > The SKILL.md installation section uses 'sympy>=1.14' (minimum version pin) rather than an exact version pin for SymPy, and recommends installing numpy, scipy, and matplotlib without any version constraints at all. - **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — parse_expr() Uses eval Internally — Code Injection Risk on Unsanitized Input
  > The references/code-generation-printing.md file documents and demonstrates use of SymPy's parse_expr(), which calls Python's eval() internally. While the reference file does include security warnings and recommends using local_dict and standard_transformations, it also provides example patterns (Pattern 3: Interactive Computation) that could be misapplied by the agent if it follows the pattern with actual user-supplied input rather than trusted literals. The file explicitly warns 'never use it on unsanitized input' but the agent may not consistently enforce this boundary when generating code for users.
  > File: `references/code-generation-printing.md`
  > **Remediation:** The reference file already includes good security guidance. Ensure the SKILL.md instructions explicitly direct the agent to never pass raw user input to parse_expr() without validation, and to prefer building expressions programmatically from typed SymPy objects (symbols(), Integer(), operators) rather than parsing strings from user input.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — File Write Operations Without Explicit User Confirmation Guidance
  > The references/code-generation-printing.md file includes patterns that write files to disk (output.tex, output.txt, output.py, document.tex, *.c files in batch generation). The skill declares allowed-tools including Write, so this is not a tool restriction violation, but the instructions do not specify that the agent should confirm file write locations with the user before writing. This could result in unintended file creation in user directories.
  > File: `references/code-generation-printing.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add guidance in SKILL.md instructions that the agent should confirm file output paths with the user before writing files, especially in batch operations. Specify that file writes should be limited to the current working directory or a user-specified output directory.

This exposes users to supply chain risk if a malicious or broken package version is published to PyPI. The risk is moderate given that these are well-known packages (Google's timesfm, PyTorch), but best practice is to pin exact versions.
  > File: `SKILL.md`

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Environment Variable Access in System Checker
  > The check_system.py script reads the HF_HOME environment variable to determine the Hugging Face cache directory. While this is a legitimate use (determining where model weights are cached), the static analyzer flagged it as part of a cross-file environment variable access pattern. In context, this is benign: the value is only used to construct a local path for disk space checking, not transmitted externally. No actual exfiltration occurs.
  > File: `scripts/check_system.py`
  > **Remediation:** This is a false positive in context. The environment variable is used only for local path resolution. No remediation required, but documentation could clarify that HF_HOME is read solely for disk space estimation.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Model Weights Downloaded from External Source Without Integrity Verification
  > The skill downloads ~800MB model weights from HuggingFace on first use without any hash/checksum verification. While HuggingFace is a reputable source and the model IDs are from Google's official namespace, there is no integrity check performed after download. If the HuggingFace CDN or DNS were compromised, malicious weights could be loaded silently.
  > File: `scripts/forecast_csv.py`
  > **Remediation:** Document that model integrity relies on HuggingFace's safetensors format (which includes metadata). Consider adding a note to verify the model SHA after download using 'huggingface-cli' or checking the model card hash.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — No Upper Bound on Batch Size or Horizon Parameters
  > The forecast_csv.py script accepts --horizon and --batch-size as user-controlled integers with no upper bound validation. A user could pass extremely large values (e.g., --horizon 1000000) that could cause excessive memory consumption or very long compute times. The system preflight check verifies RAM before model load but does not validate forecast parameters.
  > File: `scripts/forecast_csv.py`
  > **Remediation:** Add reasonable upper bounds for --horizon (e.g., max 16384 per TimesFM 2.5 context limit) and --batch-size. Validate inputs before passing to the model: 'if args.horizon > 16384: parser.error("horizon exceeds model maximum")'

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Multiple Missing Referenced Files
  > The SKILL.md references numerous files across multiple directory paths (templates/, assets/, references/) that do not exist in the package. Files such as templates/response-format.md, assets/parameters.md, templates/api-basics.md, assets/datasets-debt.md, assets/api-basics.md, templates/datasets-debt.md, templates/datasets-interest-rates.md, assets/response-format.md, assets/datasets-interest-rates.md, assets/datasets-securities.md, assets/datasets-fiscal.md, templates/datasets-fiscal.md, templates/datasets-securities.md, assets/examples.md, and templates/examples.md are all referenced but not found.
